          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Place_in_history">
        Place in history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Place in history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Herodotus announced the purpose and scope of his work at the beginning of his
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [a]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as such:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Here are presented the results of the enquiry carried out by Herodotus of Halicarnassus. The purpose is to prevent the traces of human events from being erased by time, and to preserve the fame of the important and remarkable achievements produced by both Greeks and non-Greeks; among the matters covered is, in particular, the cause of the hostilities between Greeks and non-Greeks.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Herodotus,
         <i>
          The Histories
         </i>
         <br/>
         Robin Waterfield translation (2008)
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessors">
        Predecessors
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Predecessors">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       His record of the achievements of others was an achievement in itself, though the extent of it has been debated. Herodotus's place in history and his significance may be understood according to the traditions within which he worked. His work is the earliest Greek prose to have survived intact. However,
       <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus" title="Dionysius of Halicarnassus">
        Dionysius of Halicarnassus
       </a>
       , a literary critic of
       <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">
        Augustan Rome
       </a>
       , listed seven predecessors of Herodotus, describing their works as simple, unadorned accounts of their own and other cities and people, Greek or foreign, including popular legends, sometimes melodramatic and naïve, often charming – all traits that can be found in the work of Herodotus himself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern historians regard the chronology as uncertain, but according to the ancient account, these predecessors included Dionysius of Miletus, Charon of Lampsacus,
       <a href="/wiki/Hellanicus_of_Lesbos" title="Hellanicus of Lesbos">
        Hellanicus of Lesbos
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xanthus_of_Lydia" title="Xanthus of Lydia">
        Xanthus of Lydia
       </a>
       and, the best attested of them all,
       <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">
        Hecataeus of Miletus
       </a>
       . Of these, only fragments of Hecataeus's works survived, and the authenticity of these is debatable,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but they provide a glimpse into the kind of tradition within which Herodotus wrote his own
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_style">
        Writing style
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Writing style">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In his introduction to Hecataeus's work,
       <i>
        Genealogies
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1175" data-file-width="1253" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg/190px-POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg/285px-POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg/380px-POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg 2x" width="190"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:POxy_v0017_n2099_a_01_hires.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Fragment from the
         <i>
          Histories
         </i>
         VIII on
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus">
          Papyrus Oxyrhynchus
         </a>
         2099, early 2nd century AD
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Hecataeus the Milesian speaks thus: I write these things as they seem true to me; for the stories told by the Greeks are various and in my opinion absurd.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       This points forward to the "folksy" yet "international" outlook typical of Herodotus. However, one modern scholar has described the work of Hecataeus as "a curious false start to history",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       since despite his critical spirit, he failed to liberate history from myth. Herodotus mentions Hecataeus in his
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       , on one occasion mocking him for his naive genealogy and, on another occasion, quoting Athenian complaints against his handling of their national history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is possible that Herodotus borrowed much material from Hecataeus, as stated by
       <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">
        Porphyry
       </a>
       in a quote recorded by
       <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">
        Eusebius
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In particular, it is possible that he copied descriptions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">
        crocodile
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">
        hippopotamus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(mythology)" title="Phoenix (mythology)">
        phoenix
       </a>
       from Hecataeus's
       <i>
        Circumnavigation of the Known World
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Periegesis
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        Periodos ges
       </i>
       ), even misrepresenting the source as "Heliopolitans" (
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       2.73).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       But Hecataeus did not record events that had occurred in living memory, unlike Herodotus, nor did he include the oral traditions of Greek history within the larger framework of oriental history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is no proof that Herodotus derived the ambitious scope of his own work, with its grand theme of civilizations in conflict, from any predecessor, despite much scholarly speculation about this in modern times.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus claims to be better informed than his predecessors by relying on empirical observation to correct their excessive schematism. For example, he argues for continental asymmetry as opposed to the older theory of a perfectly circular earth with Europe and Asia/Africa equal in size (
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       4.36 and 4.42). However, he retains idealizing tendencies, as in his symmetrical notions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">
        Nile
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       His debt to previous authors of prose "histories" might be questionable, but there is no doubt that Herodotus owed much to the example and inspiration of poets and story-tellers. For example, Athenian tragic poets provided him with a world-view of a balance between conflicting forces, upset by the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubris" title="Hubris">
        hubris
       </a>
       of kings, and they provided his narrative with a model of episodic structure. His familiarity with Athenian tragedy is demonstrated in a number of passages echoing
       <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">
        Aeschylus
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persae" title="Persae">
         Persae
        </a>
       </i>
       , including the epigrammatic observation that the defeat of the Persian navy at
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">
        Salamis
       </a>
       caused the defeat of the land army (
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       8.68 ~
       <i>
        Persae
       </i>
       728). The debt may have been repaid by Sophocles because there appear to be echoes of
       <i>
        The Histories
       </i>
       in his plays, especially a passage in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Antigone" title="Antigone">
         Antigone
        </a>
       </i>
       that resembles Herodotus's account of the death of Intaphernes (
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       3.119 ~
       <i>
        Antigone
       </i>
       904-920).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, this point is one of the most contentious issues in modern scholarship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">
        Homer
       </a>
       was another inspirational source.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [b]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Just as Homer drew extensively on a tradition of oral poetry, sung by wandering minstrels, so Herodotus appears to have drawn on an Ionian tradition of story-telling, collecting and interpreting the oral histories he chanced upon in his travels. These oral histories often contained folk-tale motifs and demonstrated a moral, yet they also contained substantial facts relating to geography, anthropology, and history, all compiled by Herodotus in an entertaining style and format.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_and_modern_critics">
        Contemporary and modern critics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Contemporary and modern critics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is on account of the many strange stories and the folk-tales he reported that his critics in early modern times branded him "The Father of Lies".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurnHerodotus_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BurnHerodotus-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even his own contemporaries found reason to scoff at his achievement. In fact, one modern scholar
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       has wondered if Herodotus left his home in Greek
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       , migrating westwards to Athens and beyond, because his own countrymen had ridiculed his work, a circumstance possibly hinted at in an epitaph said to have been dedicated to Herodotus at one of his three supposed resting places,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thuria,_Messenia" title="Thuria, Messenia">
        Thuria
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Herodotus the son of Sphynx
        <br/>
       </p>
       <p>
        lies; in Ionic history without peer;
        <br/>
        a Dorian born, who fled from slander's brand
        <br/>
        and made in Thuria his new native land.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-22">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Yet it was in Athens where his most formidable contemporary critics could be found. In 425 BC, which is about the time that Herodotus is thought by many scholars to have died, the Athenian comic dramatist
       <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">
        Aristophanes
       </a>
       created
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Acharnians" title="The Acharnians">
         The Acharnians
        </a>
       </i>
       , in which he blames the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       on the abduction of some prostitutes – a mocking reference to Herodotus, who reported the Persians' account of their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Persian_Wars" title="The Persian Wars">
        wars with Greece
       </a>
       , beginning with the rapes of the mythical heroines
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(mythology)" title="Io (mythology)">
        Io
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(Greek_mythology)" title="Europa (Greek mythology)">
        Europa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Medea" title="Medea">
        Medea
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Helen_of_Troy" title="Helen of Troy">
        Helen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Similarly, the Athenian historian
       <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">
        Thucydides
       </a>
       dismissed Herodotus as a "logos-writer" (story-teller).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_a_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray_a-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thucydides, who had been trained in rhetoric, became the model for subsequent prose-writers as an author who seeks to appear firmly in control of his material, whereas with his frequent digressions Herodotus appeared to minimize (or possibly disguise) his authorial control.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Moreover, Thucydides developed a historical topic more in keeping with the Greek world-view: focused on the context of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">
         polis
        </a>
       </i>
       or city-state. The interplay of civilizations was more relevant to Greeks living in Anatolia, such as Herodotus himself, for whom life within a foreign civilization was a recent memory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_a_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray_a-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Before the Persian crisis, history had been represented among the Greeks only by local or family traditions. The "Wars of Liberation" had given to Herodotus the first genuinely historical inspiration felt by a Greek. These wars showed him that there was a corporate life, higher than that of the city, of which the story might be told; and they offered to him as a subject the drama of the collision between East and West. With him, the spirit of history was born into Greece; and his work, called after the nine Muses, was indeed the first utterance of
        <a href="/wiki/Clio" title="Clio">
         Clio
        </a>
        .
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <a href="/wiki/Richard_Claverhouse_Jebb" title="Richard Claverhouse Jebb">
          Richard Claverhouse Jebb
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
          <a href="#cite_note-27">
           [25]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Life">
        Life
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Life">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2727" data-file-width="1800" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/170px-Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/255px-Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/340px-Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relief_Herodotus_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Relief of Herodotus by
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Guillaume_Moitte" title="Jean-Guillaume Moitte">
          Jean-Guillaume Moitte
         </a>
         (1806),
         <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">
          Louvre
         </a>
         , Paris
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Modern scholars generally turn to Herodotus's own writing for reliable information about his life,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       supplemented with ancient yet much later sources, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">
         Suda
        </a>
       </i>
       , an 11th century encyclopaedia which possibly took its information from traditional accounts.
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        The data are so few – they rest upon such late and slight authority; they are so improbable or so contradictory, that to compile them into a biography is like building a house of cards, which the first breath of criticism will blow to the ground. Still, certain points may be approximately fixed ...
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <a href="/wiki/George_Rawlinson" title="George Rawlinson">
          George Rawlinson
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
          <a href="#cite_note-29">
           [27]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">
        Childhood
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Childhood">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Modern accounts of his life typically
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       go something like this: Herodotus was born at
       <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">
        Halicarnassus
       </a>
       around 484 BC. There is no reason to disbelieve the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       's information about his family: that it was influential and that he was the son of Lyxes and Dryo, and the brother of Theodorus, and that he was also related to
       <a href="/wiki/Panyassis" title="Panyassis">
        Panyassis
       </a>
       – an epic poet of the time. The town was within the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Persian Empire
       </a>
       at that time, making Herodotus a Persian subject,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and it may be that the young Herodotus heard local eye-witness accounts of events within the empire and of Persian preparations for the invasion of Greece, including the movements of the local fleet under the command of
       <a href="/wiki/Artemisia_I_of_Caria" title="Artemisia I of Caria">
        Artemisia I of Caria
       </a>
       . Inscriptions recently discovered at Halicarnassus indicate that her grandson
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lygdamis_II_of_Halicarnassus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lygdamis II of Halicarnassus (page does not exist)">
        Lygdamis
       </a>
       negotiated with a local assembly to settle disputes over seized property, which is consistent with a tyrant under pressure. His name is not mentioned later in the tribute list of the Athenian
       <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">
        Delian League
       </a>
       , indicating that there might well have been a successful uprising against him some time before 454 BC. The epic poet
       <a href="/wiki/Panyassis" title="Panyassis">
        Panyassis
       </a>
       – a relative of Herodotus – is reported to have taken part in a failed uprising. Herodotus expresses affection for the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">
        Samos
       </a>
       (III, 39–60), and this is an indication that he might have lived there in his youth. So it is possible that his family was involved in an uprising against Lygdamis, leading to a period of exile on
       <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">
        Samos
       </a>
       and followed by some personal hand in the tyrant's eventual fall.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotusstatue.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herodotusstatue.JPG/170px-Herodotusstatue.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herodotusstatue.JPG/255px-Herodotusstatue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herodotusstatue.JPG/340px-Herodotusstatue.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotusstatue.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The statue of Herodotus in his hometown of
         <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">
          Halicarnassus
         </a>
         , modern
         <a href="/wiki/Bodrum" title="Bodrum">
          Bodrum
         </a>
         , Turkey
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Herodotus wrote his
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionic_Greek" title="Ionic Greek">
        Ionian
       </a>
       dialect, yet he was born in Halicarnassus, which was a
       <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">
        Dorian
       </a>
       settlement. According to the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       , Herodotus learned the Ionian dialect as a boy living on the island of Samos, to which he had fled with his family from the oppressions of Lygdamis, tyrant of Halicarnassus and grandson of Artemisia. The
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       also informs us that Herodotus later returned home to lead the revolt that eventually overthrew the tyrant. Due to recent discoveries of inscriptions at Halicarnassus dated to about Herodotus's time, we now know that the Ionic dialect was used in Halicarnassus in some official documents, so there is no need to assume (like the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       ) that he must have learned the dialect elsewhere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Further, the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       is the only source which we have for the role played by Herodotus as the heroic liberator of his birthplace. That itself is a good reason to doubt such a romantic account.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_travels">
        Early travels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early travels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As Herodotus himself reveals, Halicarnassus, though a Dorian city, had ended its close relations with its Dorian neighbours after an unseemly quarrel (I, 144), and it had helped pioneer Greek trade with Egypt (II, 178). It was, therefore, an outward-looking, international-minded port within the
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">
        Persian Empire
       </a>
       , and the historian's family could well have had contacts in other countries under Persian rule, facilitating his travels and his researches.
      </p>
      <p>
       Herodotus's eye-witness accounts indicate that he traveled in Egypt in association with Athenians, probably some time after 454 BC or possibly earlier, after an Athenian fleet had assisted the uprising against
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Persian_Egypt" title="History of Persian Egypt">
        Persian rule
       </a>
       in 460–454 BC. He probably traveled to
       <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">
        Tyre
       </a>
       next and then down the
       <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">
        Euphrates
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
        Babylon
       </a>
       . For some reason, possibly associated with local politics, he subsequently found himself unpopular in Halicarnassus, and some time around 447 BC, migrated to
       <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">
        Periclean Athens
       </a>
       – a city whose people and democratic institutions he openly admires (V, 78). Athens was also the place where he came to know the local topography (VI, 137; VIII, 52–5), as well as leading citizens such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcmaeonids" title="Alcmaeonids">
        Alcmaeonids
       </a>
       , a clan whose history features frequently in his writing.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">
        Eusebius
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
        Plutarch
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus was granted a financial reward by the Athenian assembly in recognition of his work. It is possible that he unsuccessfully applied for Athenian citizenship, a rare honour after 451 BC, requiring two separate votes by a well-attended assembly.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">
        Later life
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Later life">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 443 BC or shortly afterwards, he migrated to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thurium" title="Thurium">
        Thurium
       </a>
       as part of an Athenian-sponsored colony.
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       refers to a version of
       <i>
        The Histories
       </i>
       written by "Herodotus of Thurium", and some passages in the
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       have been interpreted as proof that he wrote about southern Italy from personal experience there (IV, 15,99; VI, 127). Intimate knowledge of some events in the first years of the
       <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">
        Peloponnesian War
       </a>
       (VI, 91; VII, 133, 233; IX, 73) indicate that he might have returned to Athens, in which case it is possible that he died there during an outbreak of the plague. Possibly he died in
       <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">
        Macedonia
       </a>
       instead, after obtaining the patronage of the court there; or else he died back in Thurium. There is nothing in the
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       that can be dated to later than 430 BC with any certainty, and it is generally assumed that he died not long afterwards, possibly before his sixtieth year.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Author_and_orator">
        Author and orator
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Author and orator">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Herodotus would have made his researches known to the larger world through oral recitations to a public crowd. John Marincola writes in his introduction to the Penguin edition of
       <i>
        The Histories
       </i>
       that there are certain identifiable pieces in the early books of Herodotus's work which could be labeled as "performance pieces". These portions of the research seem independent and "almost detachable", so that they might have been set aside by the author for the purposes of an oral performance. The intellectual matrix of the 5th century, Marincola suggests, comprised many oral performances in which philosophers would dramatically recite such detachable pieces of their work. The idea was to criticize previous arguments on a topic and emphatically and enthusiastically insert their own in order to win over the audience.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It was conventional in Herodotus's day for authors to "publish" their works by reciting them at popular festivals. According to
       <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">
        Lucian
       </a>
       , Herodotus took his finished work straight from Anatolia to the Olympic Games and read the entire
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       to the assembled spectators in one sitting, receiving rapturous applause at the end of it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a very different account by an ancient grammarian,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus refused to begin reading his work at the festival of Olympia until some clouds offered him a bit of shade – by which time the assembly had dispersed. (Hence the proverbial expression "Herodotus and his shade" to describe someone who misses an opportunity through delay.) Herodotus's recitation at Olympia was a favourite theme among ancient writers, and there is another interesting variation on the story to be found in the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       : that of
       <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Photius)" title="Bibliotheca (Photius)">
        Photius
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tzetzes" title="Tzetzes">
        Tzetzes
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in which a young Thucydides happened to be in the assembly with his father, and burst into tears during the recital. Herodotus observed prophetically to the boy's father, "Your son's soul yearns for knowledge."
      </p>
      <p>
       Eventually, Thucydides and Herodotus became close enough for both to be interred in Thucydides' tomb in Athens. Such at least was the opinion of Marcellinus in his
       <i>
        Life of Thucydides
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the
       <i>
        Suda
       </i>
       , he was buried in Macedonian
       <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">
        Pella
       </a>
       and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">
        agora
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thurium" title="Thurium">
        Thurium
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reliability">
        Reliability
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reliability">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="927" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg/170px-Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg/255px-Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg/340px-Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dedication_page_for_the_Historiae_by_Herodotus_printed_at_Venice_1494.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Dedication" title="Dedication">
          Dedication
         </a>
         in the
         <i>
          Histories
         </i>
         , translated into Latin by
         <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">
          Lorenzo Valla
         </a>
         , Venice 1494
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Histories
       </i>
       were occasionally criticized in antiquity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [c]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but modern historians and philosophers generally take a positive view.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_b_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray_b-46">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite the controversy,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [d]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus still serves as the primary, and often only, source for events in the Greek world, Persian Empire, and the region generally in the two centuries leading up until his own day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurnHerodotus_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BurnHerodotus-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jones-50">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus, like many ancient historians, preferred an element of show
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [e]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to purely analytic history, aiming to give pleasure with "exciting events, great dramas, bizarre exotica."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joe_Saltzman_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Joe_Saltzman-53">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As such, certain passages
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have been the subject of controversy and even some doubt, both in antiquity and today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lloyd_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lloyd-56">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-57">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baragwanath_19_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baragwanath_19-58">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fehling_13_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fehling_13-59">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marincola_34_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marincola_34-60">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalley_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dalley-61">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalley_2013_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dalley_2013-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [f]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The accuracy of the works of Herodotus has been controversial since his own era.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [g]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">
        Cicero
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [h]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">
        Josephus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Duris_of_Samos" title="Duris of Samos">
        Duris of Samos
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [i]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Harpocration" title="Harpocration">
        Harpocration
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [j]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
        Plutarch
       </a>
       all commented on this controversy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Generally, however, he was regarded as reliable in antiquity, and is especially so today. Many scholars, ancient and modern, routinely cite Herodotus (e.g., Aubin,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A._H._L._Heeren" title="A. H. L. Heeren">
        A. H. L. Heeren
       </a>
       , Davidson,
       <a href="/wiki/Cheikh_Anta_Diop" title="Cheikh Anta Diop">
        Cheikh Anta Diop
       </a>
       , Poe, Welsby, Celenko, Volney,
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Montet" title="Pierre Montet">
        Pierre Montet
       </a>
       , Bernal, Jackson, DuBois,
       <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
        Strabo
       </a>
       ). Many of these scholars (Welsby, Heeren, Aubin, Diop, etc.) explicitly mention the reliability of Herodotus's work
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [k]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and demonstrate corroboration of Herodotus's writings by modern scholars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [l]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A. H. L. Heeren quoted Herodotus throughout his work and provided corroboration by scholars regarding several passages (source of the Nile, location of Meroe, etc.).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To further his work on the Egyptians and Assyrians, Aubin uses Herodotus's accounts in various passages and defends Herodotus's position. Aubin said that Herodotus was "the author of the first important narrative history of the world".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Diop provides several examples (the inundations of the Nile) which, he argues, support his view that Herodotus was "quite scrupulous, objective, scientific for his time." Diop argues that Herodotus "always distinguishes carefully between what he has seen and what he has been told." Diop also notes that Strabo corroborated Herodotus's ideas about the Black Egyptians, Ethiopians, and Colchians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="788" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/220px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/330px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/440px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Reconstruction of the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oikoumene" title="Oikoumene">
          Oikoumene
         </a>
         (inhabited world), ancient map based on Herodotus, c.
         <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
          450 BC
         </span>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">
        Egypt
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Egypt">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The reliability of Herodotus' writing about Egypt is sometimes criticized.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalley_2013_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dalley_2013-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [m]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alan B. Lloyd argues that, as a historical document, the writings of Herodotus are seriously defective, and that he was working from "inadequate sources".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lloyd_56-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lloyd-56">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nielsen writes: "Though we cannot entirely rule out the possibility of Herodotus having been in Egypt, it must be said that his narrative bears little witness to it."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen_57-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-57">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       German historian Detlev Fehling questions whether Herodotus ever traveled up the Nile River, and considers doubtful almost everything that he says about Egypt and Ethiopia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baragwanath_19_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baragwanath_19-58">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fehling states that "there is not the slightest bit of history behind the whole story" about the claim of Herodotus that Pharaoh
       <a href="/wiki/Sesostris" title="Sesostris">
        Sesostris
       </a>
       campaigned in Europe, and that he left a colony in Colchia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marincola_34_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marincola_34-60">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fehling_13_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fehling_13-59">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [n]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Science">
        Science
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Science">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goldinpan.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="400" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Goldinpan.jpg/170px-Goldinpan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Goldinpan.jpg/255px-Goldinpan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Goldinpan.jpg/340px-Goldinpan.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goldinpan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Gold dust and nuggets
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Herodotus provides much information about the nature of the world and the status of science during his lifetime, often engaging in private speculation. For example, he reports that the annual flooding of the
       <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">
        Nile
       </a>
       was said to be the result of melting snows far to the south, and he comments that he cannot understand how there can be snow in Africa, the hottest part of the known world, offering an elaborate explanation based on the way that desert winds affect the passage of the Sun over this part of the world (2:18ff). He also passes on reports from
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
        Phoenician
       </a>
       sailors that, while circumnavigating
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       , they "saw the sun on the right side while sailing westwards". Owing to this brief mention, which is included almost as an afterthought, it has been argued that Africa was circumnavigated by ancient seafarers, for this is precisely where the sun ought to have been. His accounts of India are among the oldest records of Indian civilization by an outsider.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Discoveries made since the end of the 19th century have generally added to Herodotus's credibility. He described
       <a href="/wiki/Gelonus" title="Gelonus">
        Gelonus
       </a>
       , located in
       <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">
        Scythia
       </a>
       , as a city thousands of times larger than
       <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">
        Troy
       </a>
       ; this was widely disbelieved until it was rediscovered in 1975. The archaeological study of the now-submerged
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        ancient Egyptian
       </a>
       city of
       <a href="/wiki/Heracleion" title="Heracleion">
        Heracleion
       </a>
       and the recovery of the so-called "Naucratis stela" give credibility to Herodotus's previously unsupported claim that Heracleion was founded during the Egyptian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">
        New Kingdom
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="1000" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg/220px-Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg/330px-Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg/440px-Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Claude_Vignon_-_Parable_of_the_Unforgiving_Servant.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Croesus Receiving Tribute from a Lydian Peasant
         </i>
         , by
         <a href="/wiki/Claude_Vignon" title="Claude Vignon">
          Claude Vignon
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After journeys to India and Pakistan, French ethnologist
       <a href="/wiki/Michel_Peissel" title="Michel Peissel">
        Michel Peissel
       </a>
       claimed to have discovered an animal species that may illuminate one of the most bizarre passages in Herodotus's
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peissel_Ants_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Peissel_Ants-86">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Book 3, passages 102 to 105, Herodotus reports that a species of fox-sized, furry "
       <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">
        ants
       </a>
       " lives in one of the far eastern, Indian provinces of the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Persian Empire
       </a>
       . This region, he reports, is a sandy desert, and the sand there contains a wealth of fine gold dust. These giant ants, according to Herodotus, would often unearth the gold dust when digging their mounds and tunnels, and the people living in this province would then collect the precious dust. Peissel reports that, in an isolated region of northern Pakistan on the
       <a href="/wiki/Deosai_National_Park" title="Deosai National Park">
        Deosai Plateau
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilgit%E2%80%93Baltistan" title="Gilgit–Baltistan">
        Gilgit–Baltistan
       </a>
       province, there is a species of
       <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">
        marmot
       </a>
       – the
       <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_marmot" title="Himalayan marmot">
        Himalayan marmot
       </a>
       , a type of burrowing
       <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">
        squirrel
       </a>
       – that may have been what Herodotus called giant ants. The ground of the Deosai Plateau is rich in gold dust, much like the province that Herodotus describes. According to Peissel, he interviewed the
       <a href="/wiki/Brokpa" title="Brokpa">
        Minaro
       </a>
       tribal people who live in the Deosai Plateau, and they have confirmed that they have, for generations, been collecting the gold dust that the marmots bring to the surface when they are digging their underground burrows. Later authors such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny the Elder
       </a>
       mentioned this story in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gold_mining" title="Gold mining">
        gold mining
       </a>
       section of his
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">
         Naturalis Historia
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2592" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg/170px-Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg/255px-Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg/340px-Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himalayan_Marmot_at_Tshophu_Lake_Bhutan_091007_a.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_marmot" title="Himalayan marmot">
          Himalayan marmot
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Peissel offers the theory that Herodotus may have confused the old Persian word for "marmot" with the word for "mountain ant". Research suggests that Herodotus probably did not know any Persian (or any other language except his native Greek) and was forced to rely on many local translators when travelling in the vast multilingual Persian Empire. Herodotus did not claim to have personally seen the creatures which he described.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peissel_Ants_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Peissel_Ants-86">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus did, though, follow up in passage 105 of Book 3 with the claim that the "ants" are said to chase and devour full-grown camels.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_bias">
        Accusations of bias
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Accusations of bias">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some "calumnious fictions" were written about Herodotus in a work titled
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/On_the_Malice_of_Herodotus" title="On the Malice of Herodotus">
         On the Malice of Herodotus
        </a>
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">
        Plutarch
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Chaeronea" title="Chaeronea">
        Chaeronean
       </a>
       by birth, (or it might have been a
       <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Plutarch" title="Pseudo-Plutarch">
        Pseudo-Plutarch
       </a>
       , in this case "a great collector of slanders"), including the allegation that the historian was prejudiced against Thebes because the authorities there had denied him permission to set up a school.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Similarly, in a
       <i>
        Corinthian Oration
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dio_Chrysostom" title="Dio Chrysostom">
        Dio Chrysostom
       </a>
       (or yet another pseudonymous author) accused the historian of prejudice against
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">
        Corinth
       </a>
       , sourcing it in personal bitterness over financial disappointments
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – an account also given by
       <a href="/wiki/Marcellinus_(writer)" title="Marcellinus (writer)">
        Marcellinus
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        Life of Thucydides
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, Herodotus was in the habit of seeking out information from empowered sources within communities, such as aristocrats and priests, and this also occurred at an international level, with
       <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">
        Periclean Athens
       </a>
       becoming his principal source of information about events in Greece. As a result, his reports about Greek events are often coloured by Athenian bias against rival states –
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" title="Thebes (Greece)">
        Thebes
       </a>
       and Corinth in particular.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Histories
       </i>
       were sometimes criticized in antiquity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [o]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but modern historians and philosophers take a more positive view of Herodotus's methodology, especially those searching for a paradigm of objective historical writing. A few modern scholars have argued that Herodotus exaggerated the extent of his travels and invented his sources,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       yet his reputation continues largely intact. Herodotus is variously considered "father of comparative anthropology",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurnHerodotus_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-BurnHerodotus-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "the father of ethnography",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jones-50">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and "more modern than any other ancient historian in his approach to the ideal of total history".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OswynMurray_b_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-OswynMurray_b-46">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Herodotus.27s_use_of_sources_and_sense_of_authority">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Herodotus's_use_of_sources_and_sense_of_authority">
        Herodotus's use of sources and sense of authority
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Herodotus's use of sources and sense of authority">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is clear from the beginning of Book 1 of the
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       that Herodotus utilizes (or at least claims to utilize) various sources in his narrative. K.H. Waters relates that "Herodotos did not work from a purely Hellenic standpoint; he was accused by the patriotic but somewhat imperceptive Plutarch of being
       <i>
        philobarbaros
       </i>
       , a pro-barbarian or pro-foreigner."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Herodotus will at times relate various accounts of the same story. For example, in Book 1 he mentions both the Phoenician and the Persian accounts of Io.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, Herodotus will at time arbitrate between varying accounts: "I am not going to say that these events happened one way or the other. Rather, I will point out the man
       <i>
        who I know for a fact
       </i>
       began the wrong-doing against the Greeks."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Again, later, Herodotus claims himself as an authority: "I know this is how it happened because I heard it from the Delphians myself."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Throughout his work, Herodotus attempts to explain the actions of people. Speaking about Solon the Athenian, Herodotus states "[Solon] sailed away on the pretext of seeing the world,
       <i>
        but it was really so that he could not be compelled to repeal any of the laws he had laid down
       </i>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Again, in the story about Croesus and his son's death, when speaking of Adrastus (the man who accidentally killed Croesus' son), Herodotus states: "Adrastus ...
       <i>
        believing himself to be the most ill-fated man he had ever known
       </i>
       , cut his own throat over the grave."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While Herodotus had not met these people whom he is discussing, he claims to understand their thoughts and intentions.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mode_of_explanation">
        Mode of explanation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mode of explanation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Herodotus writes with the purpose of
       <i>
        explaining
       </i>
       ; that is, he discusses the reason for or cause of for an event. He lays this out in the proem: "This is the publication of the research of Herodotus of Halicarnassus, so that the actions of people shall not fade with time, so that the great and admirable achievements of both Greeks and barbarians shall not go unrenowned, and, among other things,
       <i>
        to set forth the reasons why they waged war on each other
       </i>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This mode of explanation traces itself all the way back to Homer,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who opened the
       <i>
        Iliad
       </i>
       by asking:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Which of the immortals set these two at each other's throats?
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Apollo,
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Zeus’ son and Leto’s, offended
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         by the warlord. Agamemnon had dishonored
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Chryses, Apollo's priest, so the god
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         struck the Greek camp with plague,
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         and the soldiers were dying of it.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
         <a href="#cite_note-102">
          [87]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Both Homer and Herodotus begin with a question of causality. In Homer's case, "who set these two at each other's throats?" In Herodotus's case, "Why did the Greeks and barbarians go to war with each other?"
      </p>
      <p>
       Herodotus's means of explanation does not necessarily posit a simple cause; rather, his explanations cover a host of potential causes and emotions. It is notable, however, that "the obligations of gratitude and revenge are the fundamental human motives for Herodotus, just as ... they are the primary stimulus to the generation of narrative itself."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some readers of Herodotus believe that his habit of tying events back to personal motives signifies an inability to see broader and more abstract reasons for action. Gould argues to the contrary that this is likely because Herodotus attempts to provide the rational reasons, as understood by his contemporaries, rather than providing more abstract reasons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_causality">
        Types of causality
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Types of causality">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Herodotus attributes cause to both divine and human agents. These are not perceived as mutually exclusive, but rather mutually interconnected. This is true of Greek thinking in general, at least from
       <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">
        Homer
       </a>
       onward.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Gould notes that invoking the supernatural in order to explain an event does not answer the question "why did this happen?" but rather "why did this happen to me?" By way of example, faulty craftsmanship is the human cause for a house collapsing. However, divine will is the reason that the house collapses at the particular moment when I am inside. It was the will of the gods that the house collapsed while a particular individual was within it, whereas it was the cause of man that the house had a weak structure and was prone to falling.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some authors, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoffrey_de_Ste-Croix" title="Geoffrey de Ste-Croix">
        Geoffrey de Ste-Croix
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Lang" title="Mabel Lang">
        Mabel Lang
       </a>
       , have argued that Fate, or the belief that "this is how it had to be," is Herodotus's ultimate understanding of causality.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Herodotus's explanation that an event "was going to happen" maps well on to Aristotelean and Homeric means of expression. The idea of "it was going to happen" reveals a "tragic discovery" associated with fifth-century drama. This tragic discovery can be seen in Homer's,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">
         Iliad
        </a>
       </i>
       as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       John Gould argues that Herodotus should be understood as falling in a long line of story-tellers, rather than thinking of his means of explanation as a "philosophy of history" or "simple causality". Thus, according to Gould, Herodotus's means of explanation is a mode of story-telling and narration that has been passed down from generations prior:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Herodotus' sense of what was 'going to happen' is not the language of one who holds a theory of historical necessity, who sees the whole of human experience as constrained by inevitability and without room for human choice or human responsibility, diminished and belittled by forces too large for comprehension or resistance; it is rather the traditional language of a teller of tales whose tale is structured by his awareness of the shape it must have and who presents human experience on the model of the narrative patterns that are built into his stories; the narrative impulse itself, the impulse towards 'closure' and the sense of an ending, is retrojected to become 'explanation'.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
         <a href="#cite_note-110">
          [95]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Herodotus_and_myth">
        Herodotus and myth
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Herodotus and myth">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although Herodotus considered his "inquiries" a serious pursuit of knowledge, he was not above relating entertaining tales derived from the collective body of myth, but he did so judiciously with regard for his
       <a href="/wiki/Historical_method" title="Historical method">
        historical method
       </a>
       , by corroborating the stories through enquiry and testing their probability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the gods never make personal appearances in his account of human events, Herodotus states emphatically that "many things prove to me that the gods take part in the affairs of man" (IX, 100).
      </p>
      <p>
       In Book One, passages 23 and 24, Herodotus relates the story of
       <a href="/wiki/Arion" title="Arion">
        Arion
       </a>
       , the renowned harp player, "second to no man living at that time," who was saved by a dolphin. Herodotus prefaces the story by noting that "a very wonderful thing is said to have happened," and alleges its veracity by adding that the "Corinthians and the Lesbians agree in their account of the matter." Having become very rich while at the court of Periander, Arion conceived a desire to sail to Italy and Sicily. He hired a vessel crewed by Corinthians, whom he felt he could trust, but the sailors plotted to throw him overboard and seize his wealth. Arion discovered the plot and begged for his life, but the crew gave him two options: that either he kill himself on the spot or jump ship and fend for himself in the sea. Arion flung himself into the water, and a dolphin carried him to shore.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Herodotus clearly writes as both historian and teller of tales. Herodotus takes a fluid position between the artistic story-weaving of Homer and the rational data-accounting of later historians. John Herrington has developed a helpful metaphor for describing Herodotus's dynamic position in the history of Western art and thought – Herodotus as centaur:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        The human forepart of the animal ... is the urbane and responsible classical historian; the body indissolubly united to it is something out of the faraway mountains, out of an older, freer and wilder realm where our conventions have no force.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
         <a href="#cite_note-113">
          [98]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Herodotus is neither a mere gatherer of data nor a simple teller of tales – he is both. While Herodotus is certainly concerned with giving accurate accounts of events, this does not preclude for him the insertion of powerful mythological elements into his narrative, elements which will aid him in expressing the truth of matters under his study. Thus to understand what Herodotus is doing in the
       <i>
        Histories
       </i>
       , we must not impose strict demarcations between the man as mythologist and the man as historian, or between the work as myth and the work as history. As James Romm has written, Herodotus worked under a common ancient Greek cultural assumption that the way events are remembered and retold (e.g. in myths or legends) produces a valid kind of understanding, even when this retelling is not entirely factual.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For Herodotus, then, it takes both myth and history to produce truthful understanding.
      </p>
