      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Herodotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 24em; -webkit-column-width: 24em; column-width: 24em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-4">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-4">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The title of Herodotus' work has been traditionally (i.e. for at least several hundred years) been translated rather roughly as
          <i>
           The Histories
          </i>
          or
          <i>
           The History
          </i>
          . The original title can be translated from the Greek as "researches" or "inquiries".
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
           <a href="#cite_note-3">
            [3]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-17">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-17">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          “In the scheme and plan of his work, in the arrangement and order of its parts, in the tone and character of the thoughts, in ten thousand little expressions and words, the Homeric student appears.”
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
           <a href="#cite_note-16">
            [15]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-45">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-45">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          See
          <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">
           Lucian
          </a>
          of Samosata, who went as far as to deny him a place among the famous on the Island of the Blessed in
          <i>
           Verae Historiae
          </i>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-49">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-49">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Some regard his works as being at least partly unreliable. Fehling writes of "a problem recognized by everybody", namely that Herodotus frequently cannot be taken at face value.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fehling_2_48-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Fehling_2-48">
            [45]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-52">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-52">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Boedeker comments on Herodotus' use of literary devices.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boedeker_51-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Boedeker-51">
            [47]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-64">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-64">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          For Detlev Fehling, the sources are simply not credible that Herodotus claims for many stories that he reports. Persian and Egyptian informants tell stories to Herodotus that dovetail neatly into Greek myths and literature, yet show no signs of knowing their own traditions. For Fehling, the only credible explanation is that Herodotus invented these sources, and that the stories themselves were concocted by Herodotus himself.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
           <a href="#cite_note-63">
            [58]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-65">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-65">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Kenton L. Sparks writes, "In antiquity, Herodotus had acquired the reputation of being unreliable, biased, parsimonious in his praise of heroes, and mendacious".
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-66">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-66">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Cicero (
          <i>
           On the Laws
          </i>
          I.5) said that the works of Herodotus were full of legends or "fables".
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-68">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-68">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Duris of Samos called Herodotus a myth-monger.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-70">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-70">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Harpocration wrote a book on "the lies of Herodotus".
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-74">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-74">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          such as on the
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">
           Nile Valley
          </a>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-75">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-75">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Welsby said that "archaeology graphically confirms Herodotus' observations."
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-81">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-81">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Herodotus claimed to have visited
          <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
           Babylon
          </a>
          . The absence of any mention of the
          <a href="/wiki/Hanging_Gardens_of_Babylon" title="Hanging Gardens of Babylon">
           Hanging Gardens of Babylon
          </a>
          in his work has attracted further attacks on his credibility. In response, Dalley has proposed that the Hanging Gardens may have been in Ninevah rather than in Babylon.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalley_61-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Dalley-61">
            [56]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-83">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-83">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Fehling concludes that the works of Herodotus are intended as fiction. Boedeker concurs that much of the content of the works of Herodotus are literary devices.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marincola_34_60-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-Marincola_34-60">
            [55]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boedeker_51-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Boedeker-51">
            [47]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-92">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-92">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          For example, they were criticized for inaccuracy by
          <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">
           Lucian
          </a>
          of Samosata, who attacked Herodotus as a liar in
          <i>
           Verae Historiae
          </i>
          and went as far as to deny him a place among the famous on the
          <a href="/wiki/Fortunate_Isles" title="Fortunate Isles">
           Island of the Blessed
          </a>
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
        References

        <li id="cite_note-32">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-32">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Dandamayev" title="Muhammad Dandamayev">
            Dandamaev, M. A.
           </a>
           (1989).
           <i>
            A Political History of the Achaemenid Empire
           </i>
           . BRILL. p. 153.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-9004091726" title="Special:BookSources/978-9004091726">
            978-9004091726
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            The ‘Father of History’, Herodotus, was born at Halicarnassus, and before his emigration to mainland Greece was a subject of the Persian empire.
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Political+History+of+the+Achaemenid+Empire&amp;rft.pages=153&amp;rft.pub=BRILL&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft.isbn=978-9004091726&amp;rft.aulast=Dandamaev&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHerodotus">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-33">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-33">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Kia, Mehrdad (2016).
           <i>
            The Persian Empire: A Historical Encyclopedia
           </i>
           . ABC-CLIO. p. 161.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1610693912" title="Special:BookSources/978-1610693912">
            978-1610693912
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            At the time of Herodotus’ birth southwestern Asia Minor, including Halicarnassus, was under Persian Achaemenid rule.
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Persian+Empire%3A+A+Historical+Encyclopedia&amp;rft.pages=161&amp;rft.pub=ABC-CLIO&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.isbn=978-1610693912&amp;rft.aulast=Kia&amp;rft.aufirst=Mehrdad&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHerodotus">
